<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Cassava genome from a wild ancestor to cultivated varieties"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-10-10"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Cassava is a major tropical food crop in the Euphorbiaceae family that has high carbohydrate production potential and adaptability to diverse environments. Here we present the draft genome sequences of a wild ancestor and a domesticated variety of cassava and comparative analyses with a partial inbred line. We identify 1,584 and 1,678 gene models specific to the wild and domesticated varieties, respectively, and discover high heterozygosity and millions of single-nucleotide variations. Our analyses reveal that genes involved in photosynthesis, starch accumulation and abiotic stresses have been positively selected, whereas those involved in cell wall biosynthesis and secondary metabolism, including cyanogenic glucoside formation, have been negatively selected in the cultivated varieties, reflecting the result of natural selection and domestication. Differences in microRNA genes and retrotransposon regulation could partly explain an increased carbon flux towards starch accumulation and reduced cyanogenic glucoside accumulation in domesticated cassava. These results may contribute to genetic improvement of cassava through better understanding of its biology. Cassava is a major source of food in tropical and subtropical regions. Here the authors sequence the genomes of wild and domesticated cassava varieties and identify genes that have been selected for and against during the evolution and domestication of this economically important crop.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

Here the authors sequence the genomes of wild and domesticated cassava varieties and identify genes that have been selected for and against during the evolution and domestication of this economically important crop."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Cassava is a major tropical food crop in the Euphorbiaceae family that has high carbohydrate production potential and adaptability to diverse environments. Here we present the draft genome sequences of a wild ancestor and a domesticated variety of cassava and comparative analyses with a partial inbred line. We identify 1,584 and 1,678 gene models specific to the wild and domesticated varieties, respectively, and discover high heterozygosity and millions of single-nucleotide variations. Our analyses reveal that genes involved in photosynthesis, starch accumulation and abiotic stresses have been positively selected, whereas those involved in cell wall biosynthesis and secondary metabolism, including cyanogenic glucoside formation, have been negatively selected in the cultivated varieties, reflecting the result of natural selection and domestication. Differences in microRNA genes and retrotransposon regulation could partly explain an increased carbon flux towards starch accumulation and reduced cyanogenic glucoside accumulation in domesticated cassava. These results may contribute to genetic improvement of cassava through better understanding of its biology.</p></div></div></section>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Cultivated cassava, <i>Manihot esculenta</i> Crantz, originated from its wild progenitor, <i>Manihot esculenta</i> ssp<i>. Flabellifolia</i>, in tropical lowlands along the southern rim of the Amazon basin, where sunlight, heat and rainfall are plentiful, and intervals of drought are common<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Allem, A. C. The origin of Manihot esculenta Crantz (Euphorbiaceae). Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 41, 133–150 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1558">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Allem, A. C. The closest wild relatives of cassava (Manihot esculenta Crantz). Euphytica 107, 1–12 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1561">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Olsen, K. M. &amp; Schaal, B. A. Evidence on the origin of cassava: phylogeography of Manihot esculenta. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 5586–5591 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1564">3</a></sup>. Domestication of cassava occurred during the period of approximately 12,000 to 7,000 years ago by indigenous South Americans, as supported by DNA sequence analysis of a single locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Olsen, K. M. &amp; Schaal, B. A. Evidence on the origin of cassava: phylogeography of Manihot esculenta. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 5586–5591 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1568">3</a></sup>, and by archaeological and fossil records<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Bartlett, A. S., Barghoorn, E. S. &amp; Berger, R. Fossil maize from panama. Science 165, 389–390 (1969)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1573">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gibbons, A. New view of early Amazonia: recent findings suggest complex culture was indigenous to the Amazon basin-upsetting some received opinions about environment and culture. Science 248, 1488–1490 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1576">5</a></sup>, resulting in the modern cassava cultivars with extraordinary characteristics including high biomass and high starch yield in near optimum environments, and tolerance to drought and barren soil. Cassava is the most important root crop worldwide and provides staple food for over 700 million people in Africa (51%), Asia (29%) and South America (20%; <a href="http://faostat.fao.org">http://faostat.fao.org</a>). As it is highly tolerant to drought and its storage roots can be preserved in soil for a few years, cassava is considered to be an important reserve of carbohydrates to relieve global famine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Raheem, D. &amp; Chukwuma, C. Foods from cassava and their relevance to Nigeria and other African countries. Agr. Hum. Values 18, 383–390 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1587">6</a></sup>. It is also an ideal feedstock crop for bioenergy, biomaterials and animal feeds because of its favourable agricultural characteristics and high-quantity and -quality starch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Balat, M. &amp; Balat, H. Recent trends in global production and utilization of bio-ethanol fuel. Appl. Energy 86, 2273–2282 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1591">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Schmitz, P. M. &amp; Kavallari, A. Crop plants versus energy plants—On the international food crisis. Bioorgan. Med. Chem. 17, 4020–4021 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1594">8</a></sup>.</p><p>The cassava genome (2<i>n</i>=36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Carvalho, R.,D. &amp; Guerra, M. Cytogenetics of Manihot esculenta Crantz (cassava) and eight related species. Hereditas 136, 159–168 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1604">9</a></sup> is highly heterozygous because of its outcrossing nature and broad tropical distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Fregene, M. A. et al. Simple sequence repeat marker diversity in cassava landraces: genetic diversity and differentiation in an asexually propagated crop. Theor. Appl. Genet. 107, 1083–1093 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1608">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Siqueira, M. V. B. M. et al. Microsatellite polymorphisms in cassava landraces from the Cerrado biome, Mato Grosso do Sul, Brazil. Biochem. Genet. 48, 879–895 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1611">11</a></sup>. Conventional breeding and marker-assisted selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Okogbenin, E. &amp; Fregene, M. Genetic mapping of QTLs affecting productivity and plant architecture in a full-sib cross from non-inbred parents in Cassava (Manihot esculenta Crantz). Theor. Appl. Genet. 107, 1452–1462 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1615">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chen, X., Xia, Z., Fu, Y., Lu, C. &amp; Wang, W. Constructing a genetic linkage map using an F1 population of non-inbred parents in cassava (Manihot esculenta Crantz). Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 28, 676–683 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1618">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rabbi, I. Y., Kulembeka, H. P., Masumba, E., Marri, P. R. &amp; Ferguson, M. An EST-derived SNP and SSR genetic linkage map of cassava (Manihot esculenta Crantz). Theor. Appl. Genet. 125, 329–342 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1621">14</a></sup> have so far proved ineffective in achieving its potential regarding desirable traits, such as high-quality starch, storage root yield, avoidance to postharvest biological deterioration and resistance to diseases. For instance, cassava storage root yield is approximately 13.6 t ha<sup>−1</sup> globally, which is two- to fourfold below its potential productivity. The lack of a reference genome sequence and other genomic and transcriptomic resources has limited progress in basic biological research and breeding in cassava. Therefore, the draft genome sequence of a partial inbred cassava line, AM560, has been generated and publicly released relatively recently<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Prochnik, S. et al. The cassava genome: current progress, future directions. Trop. Plant Biol. 5, 88–94 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1628">15</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net/cassava.php/">http://www.phytozome.net/cassava.php/</a>). The sequence integrated 26- and 0.9-fold coverage of Roche 454 and Sanger reads, resulting in 530-Mb assembled scaffolds (including 410-Mb of contigs with no gaps), that cover approximately 70% of the cassava genome.</p><p>In the present study, we sequence the genomes of two cassava genotypes: W14 (<i>Manihot esculenta</i> ssp<i>. flabellifolia</i>), a wild subspecies that shows low storage root yield and low root starch content; and KU50, a variety commonly cultivated in Southeast Asia that has six to eight times higher storage root yield potential and five to six times higher starch content than W14 as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Information</a>. Using an integrated assembly strategy combining shotgun Illumina and Roche 454 reads, and a bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC)-based physical map with BAC-end Sanger sequences, we generate a high-quality draft genome sequence of cassava using established protocols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1651">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chan, A. P. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oilseed species Ricinus communis. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 951–956 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1654">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1657">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1660">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579–584 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1663">20</a></sup>. In addition, the genome of a self-pollinated clone (S1.600) derived from the <i>sugary</i> cassava landrace CAS36 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Carvalho, L. J. C. B., de Souza, C. R. B., de Mattos Cascardo, J. C., Junior, C. B. &amp; Campos, L. Identification and characterization of a novel cassava (Manihot esculenta Crantz) clone with high free sugar content and novel starch. Plant Mol. Biol. 56, 643–659 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1670">21</a>) is sequenced to 20-fold coverage, and the transcriptomes of W14, KU50 and another cultivated variety Arg7 are profiled during plant ontogeny. A comparative analysis of the three genome sequences and annotated transcriptomes enables us to better understanding genomic features underlying the evolution and domestication of cassava<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang, Z., Gerstein, M. &amp; Snyder, M. RNA-Seq: a revolutionary tool for transcriptomics. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 57–63 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1674">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Roberts, A. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1677">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B. A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621–628 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1680">24</a></sup>, particularly in relation to carbon flux, starch synthesis and biosynthesis of cyanogenic compounds. These genomic resources and findings provide a platform for advancing basic biology research, gene discovery and genomic selection-assisted breeding in cassava<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Nakaya, A. &amp; Isobe, S. N. Will genomic selection be a practical method for plant breeding? Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 110, 1303–1316 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1684">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen, H., He, H., Zhou, F., Yu, H. &amp; Deng, X. W. Development of genomics-based genotyping platforms and their applications in rice breeding. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 16, 247–254 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1687">26</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The genomes of cassava lines, W14 and KU50 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 1</a>) were sequenced and <i>de novo</i> assembled using next-generation sequencing technologies and hybrid assembly approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, Z. et al. Comparison of the two major classes of assembly algorithms: overlap-layout-consensus and de-bruijn-graph. Brief. Funct. Genomics 11, 25–37 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1715">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 5</a>). For W14, a 432-Mb assembly with an N50 of 43 kb was obtained. The assembly spanned 58.2% of the 742-Mb cassava genome, estimated by the kmer-spectrum (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figure 2</a>), and 92% of the sequence were non-gapped contigs. For KU50, the assembly spanned 495 Mb representing 66.7% of the cassava genome. The N50 was 19 kb and 81% sequences were non-gapped contigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 5–8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 3–5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Tables 3 and 6</a>). The quality of the draft genome sequence of W14 was evaluated by aligning the Roche 454 sequences of five BAC clones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). In this way, we determined that the average error rate between the BAC and the draft genome sequence was less than 0.61% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 5</a>). The detected errors were single-nucleotide mismatches and insertions/deletions. These results show that the W14 draft genome sequence assembly is of high quality in spite of its high heterozygosity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 11</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 9</a>) and complexity. The genome coverage and quality of these assemblies were further confirmed by 201,392 available transcript sequences of W14 and KU50. Specifically, 94.9% and 92.8% of the transcripts could be aligned to the genome assemblies of W14 and KU50, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 7, 8 and 9a,b</a>). Using transcriptomics data and the <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, 34,483 and 38,845 genes were predicted in the W14 and KU50 genomes, respectively. Comparison to protein databases, predicted 33,310 (96.6%) protein-coding genes in W14 and 37,592 (96.8%) in KU50 (using <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>) and tentative functions could be assigned (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 9 and 10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 10, 12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><p>The genome sequence assembly was searched for repetitive DNA using <i>de novo</i> approaches that identified 36.9% and 25.7% of the W14 and KU50 genomes as repetitive sequences, respectively. The majority of the repetitive elements were long interspersed nuclear elements and long-terminal repeat elements (LTRs, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 8</a>). These results, in addition to the fact that around 35% of the genome could not be assembled, suggest that the cassava genome is highly heterochromatic. This was confirmed by chromosome <i>in-situ</i> hybridization using an LTR probe (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Cassava comparative genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Cassava comparative genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6110/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6110/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6110_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6110/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6110_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="673" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Venn diagram of SNVs/InDels diversity of the cassava genomes of W14, KU50 and CAS36 sequenced in this study with comparison to the AM560 genome sequences previously released. The number of SNVs is listed and the number of InDels is shown in parentheses. (<b>b</b>) Chromosome <i>in situ</i> hybridization showing the repeated occurrence of 45S (Nucleolus organizer, NOR), LTR and chromosome numbers (2<i>n</i>=36) of cultivar KU50. (<b>c</b>) A CirCOS (<a href="http://circos.ca/">http://circos.ca/</a>) figure showing synteny between three paralogous cassava genomic regions and their putative orthologues present in <i>R. communis</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes. Coloured lines connect the cassava scaffolds to the <i>A. thaliana</i> chromosomes and <i>R. communis</i> scaffolds. The line distances across different scaffolds denote the similarities of the segments, with a longer line indicating a higher similarity. (<b>d</b>) Gene tree showing the divergence time of the wild ancestor subspecies to cultivars, referenced to neighbour species in the Euphorbiaceae family inferred from sequence comparison to 71 chloroplast genes from eight different plant species. Mtr: <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, Csa: <i>Cucumis sativus</i>, Ptr: <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, Pni: <i>Populus nigra</i>, Ptd: <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> x <i>Populus deltoids</i>, Rco: <i>Ricinus communis</i>, Ees: <i>Euphorbia esula</i>, Jcu: <i>Jatropha curcas</i>, Mef-W14: <i>Manihot esculenta</i> ssp. <i>flabellifolia</i> (W14), Mes-KU50: <i>Manihot esculenta</i> ssp. <i>esculenta</i> (KU50), Mes-AM560: <i>Manihot esculenta</i> ssp. <i>esculenta</i> (cultivar AM560).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6110/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The level of heterozygosity in cassava is among the highest found in sequenced plant genomes, as determined by the frequency of single-nucleotide variations (SNVs) and insertions and deletions (InDels) in its genome. We identified 3.8 and 3.4 SNVs per kilo-base (kb) in the W14 and the KU50 genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 9</a>), which are much higher levels than those found in bamboo (1.0 per kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Peng, Z. et al. The draft genome of the fast-growing non-timber forest species moso bamboo (Phyllostachys heterocycla). Nat. Genet. 45, 456–461 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1899">28</a></sup>, peach (1.5 per kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="International Peach Genome Initiative. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487–494 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1903">29</a></sup> and poplar (2.6 per kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1907">30</a></sup>, while they are comparable to those of grape (4.2 per kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Velasco, R. et al. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS ONE 2, e1326 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1911">31</a></sup> and potato (4.3 per kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1916">18</a></sup>. Cassava’s high heterozygosity may have important implications for the severe inbreeding depression observed in this crop.</p><p>Comparative genomics analysis revealed a considerable amount of genome diversity (SNVs and InDels) in W14, KU50 and CAS36 when compared with the reference genome of AM560. We identified 6.9 SNVs and 0.8 InDels per kb in W14, whereas 0.7 SNVs and 0.08 InDels per kb in the KU50. The number of SNVs discovered by comparison between the W14, KU50, CAS36 and AM560 genomes ranged from 2.84 to 4.81 millions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Tables 4, 10, 11 and 12</a>). Of these, 570,695 were shared by the genomes of wild and cultivated varieties, and 200,908 were found in genic regions. These SNVs constitute valuable markers for genotyping, genetic analysis and genomics-based breeding in cassava (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 12</a>).</p><p>A total of 63 microRNA (miRNA) families consisting of 147 miRNAs were identified in the cassava genome, including 22 previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zeng, C. et al. Conservation and divergence of microRNAs and their functions in Euphorbiaceous plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, 981–995 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d34285e1939">32</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Data 7</a>). Other noncoding RNAs, including transfer RNAs (tRNAs, 861 in W14 and 707 in KU50), ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs, including 18S, 26S, 5.8S and 5S; 337 in W14 and 192 in KU50), small nuclear RNAs, small nucleolar RNAs, signal recognition particle RNAs and long noncoding RNAs, were also found in the wild and cultivated cassava genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 21</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome variation</h3><p>Alignment of the larger scaffolds revealed that there is a significant similarity among the three cassava genomes, as expected, more substantial syntenic blocks are found between <i>M. esculenta</i> and <i>Ricinus communis</i> than between <i>M. esculenta</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). Of the 15,636 gene families identified in <i>M. esculenta</i>, 2,043 were present in cassava but absent in other sequenced Euphorbiaceae genomes (<i>R. communis</i> and <i>Jatropha curcas</i>) or the outgroup species <i>(Vitis vinifera</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Further gene model comparisons among cassava and 12 more distantly related genomes revealed that 8,414 gene models were unique to cassava and 3,710 were specific to Euphorbiaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 14 and 15</a>). Using 71 chloroplast genes from eight different plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>), we estimated that cassava diverged from rubber tree (<i>Hevea braziliensis</i>) 5.1 million years ago (MYA), 6.4 MYA from physic nut (<i>J. curcas</i>) and 14.8 MYA from castor bean (<i>R. communis</i>). Therefore, the cassava lineage from which cultivated cassava was originated diverged from a common ancestor approximately 0.5 MYA. These results are in agreement with the reported divergence time between <i>Manihot</i> and other genera in the Euphorbiaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chacón, J., Madriñán, S., Debouck, D., Rodriguez, F. &amp; Tohme, J. Phylogenetic patterns in the genus Manihot (Euphorbiaceae) inferred from analyses of nuclear and chloroplast DNA regions. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 49, 260–267 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2020">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Simon, M. F. et al. Recent assembly of the Cerrado, a neotropical plant diversity hotspot, by in situ evolution of adaptations to fire. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 20359–20364 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2023">34</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 13</a>).</p><p>We compared all the predicted genes from the genomes of W14, KU50 and AM560. A total of 28,302 independent gene models were confirmed, although copy number variations existed and were more frequently observed in the cultivated varieties than in W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Data 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary 16</a>). Among the gene models, 1,584 were unique to W14 or lost in KU50 and AM560, whereas another 1,678 genes were specific to the cultivated varieties, and 20,133 homologous genes (including 16,219 high-confidence orthologues) were shared among the three draft genome sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 14</a>). The majority of the present and absent variation genes could be assigned to six Gene Ontology (GO) functional categories, including ‘catalytic activity’, ‘binding’, ‘metabolic process’, ‘cellular process’, ‘cell’ and ‘cell part’, and those genes with significant copy number variation were mainly ascribed into the first three functional categories (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 17–19 and 41–43</a>). Significant differences in the average SNVs were detected between W14 and the cultivated varieties (1.7%) and between the two cultivars (0.5%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>).</p><p>The synonymous (<i>Ks</i>) and nonsynonymous substitution rate (<i>Ka</i>) and selection pressure (<i>Ka/Ks</i>) of the gene set were used to describe evolutionary signatures of the cassava genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Qiu, Q. et al. The yak genome and adaptation to life at high altitude. Nat. Genet 44, 946–949 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2064">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jain, M. et al. A draft genome sequence of the pulse crop chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.). Plant J. 74, 715–729 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2067">36</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 26</a>). Approximately 2,818 genes were strictly positively selected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i>&gt;1), 436 genes were negatively selected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i>&lt;1) and 9,298 genes were selection-neutral (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i>=1) during evolution of cultivated varieties, whereas 6,342 genes exhibited lack of neutral or selected divergence between cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <i>Ka</i>=<i>Ks</i>=0, <i>Ka</i>=0, <i>Ks</i>&gt;0 and <i>Ka</i>&gt;0, <i>Ks</i>=0) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Tables 13, 14 and 15</a>). By comparison, we found that 1,133 genes have been heavily selected in the domesticated cultivar, indicating a selective sweep. Analyses of GO functional categories indicated that those genes were mainly enriched in four categories: (i) ‘developmental process’ including cell differentiation and organ development such as leaf, stem, storage root and fruit; (ii) ‘metabolic process’ centred around cell wall polysaccharide synthesis, secondary metabolites and fatty acid metabolism; (iii) ‘biological regulation’ involved in regulation of cell size, cellular metabolism, immune and transcription; (iv) ‘response to stimulus’ including abiotic stresses such as light, temperature, water and oxygen, and biotic stresses caused by viral, bacterial and fungal, and response to hormones such as abscisic acid, ethylene, jasmonic acid and brassinosteroids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>). The enrichments in such GO categories suggested that those genes that underwent selection cover nearly every aspect of phenotypic variations necessary for cassava cultivation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Selection pressure and carbon flux diversification in cassava."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Selection pressure and carbon flux diversification in cassava.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6110/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6110/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6110_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6110/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6110_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="980" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Chart for synonymous substitution (<i>Ks</i>) and nonsynonymous substitution rate (<i>Ka</i>) and selection pressure (<i>Ka/Ks</i>) between wild W14 and cultivated variety (WC) and between cultivated varieties (CC). <i>Ka/Ks</i>=1 indicates genes with neutral selection, <i>Ka/Ks</i>&gt;1 indicates positive selection and <i>Ka/Ks</i>&lt;1 indicates negative selection. (<b>b</b>) The differential expression patterns of genes involved in photosynthesis, Calvin cycle, sugar transport and starch synthesis in storage roots and leaves between cultivated varieties (KU50 and Arg7) and wild ancestor (W14) revealed by digital transcriptome sequencing. (<b>c</b>) A model of high-efficient starch accumulation in the tuber roots of domesticated cassava. Red arrows present the carbon flux directions in cultivar and blue arrows indicate the carbon flux directions in wild W14. The width of the arrow indicates the strength of carbon flux. The gene symbol marked in red shows genes with copy number expansion in cultivars. cPGM, cytoplasmic phosphor-glucomutase; GPI, glucose-6-phosphate isomerase; G6PT, glucose-6-phosphate/phosphate translocator; pPGM, phospho-glucomutase; SBE, starch branching enzyme; SS, starch synthase; SUT, sucrose transporter; TPT, triosephosphate translocator; UTP, uridine triphosphate.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6110/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparative transcriptome analysis between W14 and cultivated varieties in developing leaf and storage root revealed additional specific features related to the evolution of the cassava varieties. From the total of 31,396 genes expressed in W14 or cultivated varieties, 749 show significantly differential expression in leaves and 2,732 in storage roots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 28</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 16</a>). GO analysis of those genes revealed an enrichment in genes involved in specific metabolic pathways in the wild and cultivated varieties. In leaves, the cultivated varieties show a particular transcript enrichment in genes involved in ‘photosynthesis’ and shaping the photosynthetic organelles. Interestingly, genes belonging to the GO category of ‘response to stimulus’, including abiotic and biotic stresses are also enriched in the cultivated varieties (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 30</a>). In contrast, genes involved in ‘transporter activity’, including a potassium symporter and a calcium transporting ATPase, are enriched in the wild W14. The category ‘positive regulation of flower’ was specifically enriched in W14, which is consistent with the fact that sexual reproduction is more frequent in wild than in cultivated cassava. In storage roots, genes included in the categories ‘cell part’ (specially subcategories of ‘cytoplast’ and ‘plasmid organelle’) and ‘response to stimulus’ (particularly abscisic acid, oxidative stress and temperature) were only enriched in cultivated varieties. However, genes within the categories ‘cell wall polysaccharide biosynthesis process’, ‘secondary metabolic process’ and ‘response to stimulus’ (such as water stress and jasmonic acid) were enriched in the wild species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 29</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 17</a>). These enriched GO categories in the transcriptomes of wild and cultivated varieties were consistent with the functions of the genes found to be under higher selection pressure (<i>Ka/Ks</i>). Further statistical analysis revealed that the Ka/Ks ratios of genes belonging to GO categories enriched in the cultivated varieties were higher than those from categories in the W14 wild species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 31</a>). Those genes have been restrictedly selected and differentially expressed between wild and cultivars, probably geared the ecological changes resulting a shift in growth environment from rainforest to cerrados. This is consistent with the observed variation of the phenotypes of domesticated cassava.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Carbon flux diversification</h3><p>The high carbon accumulation in the form of starch in the storage root is an extraordinary feature of cultivated cassava. The transcriptome annotation showed that a considerable number of genes involved in photosynthesis and the Calvin cycle in leaves, and sucrose transport and starch synthesis in storage roots were preferentially expressed in the two domesticated varieties when compared with the wild W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). This is consistent with the higher vigour and yield potential showed by KU50 and Arg7 relative to W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). These results were confirmed by reverse transcriptase–quantitative PCR of selected genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 34</a>). Also, we found an alternative starch synthesis pathway relying on plastid phosphorylase (<i>Pho1</i>), which was expressed at a higher level in cultivated varieties than in W14 in the storage roots. This pathway allows glucose 1-phosphate to be directly transferred into amyloplasts, as shown in rice grain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Satoh, H. et al. Mutation of the plastidial α-glucan phosphorylase gene in rice affects the synthesis and structure of starch in the endosperm. Plant Cell 20, 1833–1849 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2244">37</a></sup> and potato tubers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Fettke, J. et al. Glucose 1-phosphate is efficiently taken up by potato (Solanum tuberosum) tuber parenchyma cells and converted to reserve starch granules. N. Phytol. 185, 663–675 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2248">38</a></sup>. The expression level of genes involved in cell wall synthesis and secondary metabolism are significantly decreased in leaves and storage roots of cultivated varieties in comparison to the wild subspecies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 32–33</a>). These results agree with the observation that SWEET genes controlling sucrose efflux into the cell wall show reduced expression in KU50 and Arg7, but not in W14. At the genome level, copy number expansion and alternative splicing were found in several key genes in the cultivars, such as aldolase, phosphoglycerate kinase and ribulose bisphosphate carboxylase, which are involved in photosynthesis. Genes involved in starch synthesis and accumulation in amyloplasts of storage roots such as sucrose transporters, sucrose synthases, ADP glucose pyrophosphorylase (APL), starch branching enzymes and phospho-glucomutase showed similar copy number and alternative splicing differences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Data 5</a>). These genes have been identified as the key genes strongly associated with cassava storage root development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yang, J., An, D. &amp; Zhang, P. Expression profiling of cassava storage roots reveals an active process of glycolysis/gluconeogenesis. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 53, 193–211 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2262">39</a></sup>. Furthermore, our result suggests that miRNAs may play a role in regulating storage root formation and growth as well as starch synthesis. At least nine miRNAs could target genes that were highly expressed in the storage roots of cultivars and were involved in the photosynthesis and carbon metabolism pathways (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Fig. 36</a>) as observed in other plants. For example, miR394 directly downregulates APL2, a key gene in starch synthesis at the late stage of storage root development in cultivated varieties; likewise miR319, miR159, miR160, miR166 and miR396 negatively regulate their targets, such as MYB33 and ARF10, which control starch synthesis through ABA signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hu, Y.-F. et al. Binding of ABI4 to a CACCG motif mediates the ABA-induced expression of the ZmSSI gene in maize (Zea mays L.) endosperm. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 5979–5989 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2275">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Peng, T. et al. Characterization and expression patterns of microRNAs involved in rice grain filling. PLoS ONE 8, e54148 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2278">41</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Data 9</a>). MiR167, miR169 and miR156 positively regulate transcription factors RD19, NF-YA3 and SPL13B, respectively, which are involved in storage root and leaf development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bernoux, M. et al. RD19, an Arabidopsis cysteine protease required for RRS1-R-mediated resistance, is relocalized to the nucleus by the ralstonia solanacearum PopP2 effector. Plant Cell 20, 2252–2264 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2286">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Nardini, M. et al. Sequence-specific transcription factor NF-Y displays histone-like DNA binding and H2B-like ubiquitination. Cell 152, 132–143 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2289">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Yang, Z. et al. Comparative study of SBP-box gene family in Arabidopsis and rice. Gene 407, 1–11 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2292">44</a></sup>. Remarkably, target genes such as MYB33 ARF10 and NF-YA3 are known to bind to <i>cis</i>-elements in the upstream regions of genes related to starch metabolism, such as SuSy, APL and genes involved in photosynthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Figs 35 and 37–40</a>). Taken together, the observed gene expression patterns, enhanced agronomic phenotypes, copy number expansions and miRNA- and <i>cis</i>-element-mediated regulation of key genes suggest that carbon flux could have been shifted as a result of domestication in cassava. Based on these findings, we suggest a model of the efficient accumulation of starch in cassava (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Zhou, S.-R., Yin, L.-L. &amp; Xue, H.-W. Functional genomics based understanding of rice endosperm development. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 16, 236–246 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2312">45</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Cyanogenesis differentiation</h3><p>The latent toxicity caused by cyanogenesis in cassava is clearly a potential health hazard when it is consumed as food. The pathway for cyanogenic glucoside biosynthesis in cassava and the genes encoding the enzymes involved have been elucidated in recent years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Jørgensen, K. et al. Biosynthesis of the cyanogenic glucosides linamarin and lotaustralin in cassava: isolation, biochemical characterization, and expression pattern of CYP71E7, the oxime-metabolizing cytochrome P450 enzyme. Plant Physiol. 155, 282–292 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2324">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kannangara, R. et al. Characterization and expression profile of two UDP-glucosyltransferases, UGT85K4 and UGT85K5, catalyzing the last step in cyanogenic glucoside biosynthesis in cassava. Plant J. 68, 287–301 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2327">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Gleadow, R. M. &amp; Møller, B. L. Cyanogenic glycosides: synthesis, physiology, and phenotypic plasticity. Ann. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 155–185 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2330">48</a></sup>. We determined the linamarin and lotaustralin content in cultivated KU50, Arg7 and wild W14, and found that the linamarin content was reduced six- to tenfold in storage roots and three- to fourfold in leaves of KU50 and Arg7 relative to W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 18</a>). Remarkably, the expression of the genes <i>CYP79D1, CYP79D2</i>, <i>CYP71E7, CYP71E11, UGT85K4</i> and <i>UGT85K5</i> that encode the enzymes catalysing linamarin and lotaustralin formation, all exhibited five- to tenfold lower expression levels in the storage roots and leaves of KU50 relative to W14, further suggesting a potential outcome of domestication. Different classes of DNA retrotransposons, like miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) and LTR transposable elements, have been shown to influence the expression of proximal genes, especially if simultaneously situated downstream and upstream of the same gene. In general, gene expression is suppressed by the presence of these elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Gleadow, R. M. &amp; Møller, B. L. Cyanogenic glycosides: synthesis, physiology, and phenotypic plasticity. Ann. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 155–185 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2353">48</a></sup>. To investigate potential effects of transposons on gene expression in cassava, the 1-kb upstream regions of orthologous genes present in the W14, KU50 and AM560 genomes were analysed for the presence of MITEs. A total of 553 MITEs were found, of which 310 and 243 were uniquely present within the genomes of AM560 and W14, respectively. Among the 310 AM560-specific MITE insertions, 96 (34.5%) showed significantly lower expression and 32 (11.5%) had significantly higher expressions in storage roots or leaves of cultivated varieties when compared with W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Data 6</a>). We compared the genomic regions containing <i>CYP71E11, CYP71E7</i> and <i>UGT85K4</i>, and found that these three genes were positioned in a linear array within homologous scaffolds in the three genomes. Two distinct larger insertions containing MITE and LTR transposons were identified to be present in the 5′UTR and 3′UTR regions of those genes in KU50 and AM560, but not in the wild ancestor W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Taken together, these results suggest that transposon activity may have played a role in the reduction of cyanogenic glucoside content in the domesticated cassava. It remains to be seen how the distribution patterns of transposable elements affect cyanogenic compound biosynthesis in cassava, although transposable elements have been shown to alter the expression patterns of adjacent genes in plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wang, X., Weigel, D. &amp; Smith, L. M. Transposon variants and their effects on gene expression in Arabidopsis. PLoS Genet. 9, e1003255 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2370">49</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Cyanogenesis differentiation between wild and cultivated cassava."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Cyanogenesis differentiation between wild and cultivated cassava.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6110/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6110/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6110_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6110/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6110_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="675" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Minimizing of cyanogenic glucoside content in cultivar KU50 and Arg7 relative to wild W14: over twofold in leaves and fivefold in storage root with five repeat plants. (<b>b</b>) Differential expression of genes in the cyanogenic glucoside synthesis pathway between cultivar KU50, Arg7 and wild W14 identified by RNA-seq. DS, developing stem; ETR, early storage root; LF, leaf; LTR, late storage root; MTR, medium tuber root. (<b>c</b>) A transposon regulation model of cyanogenesis in cassava: among the interval regions of three genes in a linear array as <i>CYP71E11</i>, <i>CYP71E7</i> and <i>UGT85K4</i>, there were more transposable or retrotransposable elements in the gene 1-kb upstream regions of cultivated species KU50 and AM560 than wild subspecies W14. CDS, Coding sequence.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6110/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>We produced and annotated two draft genomes of cassava, a cultivated variety and a wild ancestor. Comparative analysis provided new insights into cassava genome evolution and genetic events that may have occurred during domestication. Gene models specific to either wild or cultivated cassava were elucidated. We found a high degree of heterozygosity between the analysed cassava genomes and gene sets that have been strictly selected during the process of evolution and, potentially, domestication. Genes responding to stimulus such as light, high temperature, water stress and oxidative stress were highly expressed in domesticated cassava, most likely reflecting their adaptation to tropical and dryer growth conditions. On the other hand, some genes involved in ion membrane transport were lost, suggesting that wild cassava ancestors were more tolerant to extreme environments than the current cultivated varieties. Particularly, two parallel but contrasting selection trends were identified in the domesticated cultivars, one leading to an increase in major carbon metabolism pathways, photosynthesis, sugar transport and starch metabolism that could enhance starch yield potential, and the other leading to a dwindling of cell wall and secondary metabolism, including cyanogenic compounds. This carbon flux shift towards starch accumulation would be desirable in cultivated varieties, whereas stress tolerance may not be so critical in cultivation conditions, as biotic and abiotic stress can be milder than in natural environments. Therefore, we propose that a pathway that prioritizes starch accumulation versus cyanogenesis has been selected in cultivated cassava. In addition, the development of substantial new genomic resources, including millions of SNVs, which are available in a public database (<a href="http://www.cassava-genome.cn/">http://www.cassava-genome.cn/</a>), will promote development of toolkits for enhanced cassava breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genomic DNA isolation</h3><p>To reduce organelle contamination in genomic DNA, nuclei were isolated from fresh young leaves of W14 and KU50, as described by Zhang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Zhang, H.-B., Zhao, X., Ding, X., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabase-size DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J. 7, 175–185 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2444">50</a></sup> Briefly, approximately 100 g of tissues were ground into a fine powder in liquid nitrogen and transferred to a beaker containing ice-cold 1 × homogenization buffer plus 0.5% Triton X-100 and 0.15% β-mercaptoethanol. After filtering the homogenate through cheesecloth and Miracloth, the nuclei were washed with the same buffer and centrifuged. This step was repeated until the nuclei pellet became white. DNA was extracted from the nuclei as described by Kidwell and Osborn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kidwell, K. K. &amp; Osborn, T. C. inPlant Genomes: Methods for Genetic and Physical Mapping eds Beckman J. S., Osborn T. C. 1–13Kluwer Academic Publishers (1992)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2448">51</a></sup>. The purified DNA was dissolved in 1 ml of TE-buffer for Illumina and 454 sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Genome sequence data of W14 and KU50 were produced with the Illumina HiSeq2000 (Illumina) and Roche/454 GS FLX platforms (Roche) at the Beijing Institute of Genomics and Qingdao Bioenergy and Process Institute of the Chinese Academy of Sciences. A total of 76.32 Gb high-quality DNA sequence for W14 and 34.43 Gb for KU50 representing 103- and 46-fold coverage of the 742-Mb cassava genome, respectively, were generated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">BAC library and physic mapping</h3><p>The BAC libraries of wild W14 and cultivated cassava inbred line AM560-2 were constructed used for integrated assembly of genome W14 and KU50, respectively. For W14, <i>Eco</i>RI- and <i>Hin</i>dIII-digested DNA fragments were cloned into pCC1BAC vector and the BAC libraries were constructed by Amplicon Express Inc., using the method of Tao <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Tao, Q., Wang, A. &amp; Zhang, H.-B. One large-insert plant transformation-competent BIBAC library and three BAC libraries of Japonica rice for genome research in rice and other grasses. Theor. Appl. Genet. 105, 1058–1066 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2477">52</a></sup> Total of 59,904 clones with the average insert size of 115 kb for <i>Eco</i>RI and 129 kb for <i>Hin</i>dIII, respectively, were acquired and represent approximately ten genome equivalents. For AM560-2, the DNA fragments were cloned into the <i>Hin</i>dIII site of the vector pIndigoBAC536, and transformed into the <i>E. coli</i> host DH10B. A total of 72,192 clones were obtained with an average insert size of 115 kb and approximately 7% clones with no insert. The library represents about 11 × coverage of cassava genome size of 746 Mb estimated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Note 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 2</a>). BAC clones were fingerprinted with a SNaPshot high-information content fingerprinting method described by Luo <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Luo, M. et al. High-throughput fingerprinting of bacterial artificial chromosomes using the SNaPshot labeling kit and sizing of restriction fragments by capillary electrophoresis. Genomics 82, 378–389 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2504">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nelson, W. M. et al. Efficacy of clone fingerprinting methodologies. Genomics 89, 160–165 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2507">54</a></sup>, and modified by Gu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Gu, Y. Q. et al. Construction of physical map for Brachypodium distachyon and its comparative analysis with rice. BMC Genom. 10, 496 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2514">55</a></sup> The GeneMapper software was used for fragment size-calling. Outputs of size-calling files were automatically edited with the FP Miner programme. A total of 72,192 BAC clones from the cassava AM560-2 library and 29,952 clones from W14 library were fingerprinted, 58,244 clones of AM560-2 and 24,784 clones of W14 were suitable for fingerprinted contigs (FPC) assembly, respectively, resulted in a total of 2,105 contigs and 5,054 singletons in AM560-2 and a total of 2,485 contigs and 2,909 singletons in W14 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6110#MOESM138">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The long sequence of 454 libraries assembly was generated using Newbler version 2.5.3 with default parameters, and short sequence of illumina libraries assembly was generated using SOAP denovo version 1.05 with default parameters, artificial sequences and poor quality bases had been trimmed before input for assembly process. Then by using BLAST version 2.2.25 with <i>E</i>-value parameter as 1e-5 to filtered the repeat contigs, and using Phrap version 1.080812 to merge the contigs with overlap–layout–consensus relationship. Using Fan’s link scaffold build bridge from contigs to scaffolds with illumina hierarchical insert span paired-ends and mate-pairs. The mega scaffolds were integrated by the scaffolds and BAC paired-ends sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="van Oeveren, J. et al. Sequence-based physical mapping of complex genomes by whole genome profiling. Genome Res. 21, 618–625 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2532">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Engler, F. W., Hatfield, J., Nelson, W. &amp; Soderlund, C. A. Locating sequence on FPC maps and selecting a minimal tiling path. Genome Res. 13, 2152–2163 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2535">57</a></sup>, the connection between scaffolds and BAC paired-ends were ordered by FPC map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Nelson, W. &amp; Soderlund, C. Integrating sequence with FPC fingerprint maps. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, e36 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2539">58</a></sup> using GATE v1.0 (<a href="https://github.com/BENMFeng">https://github.com/BENMFeng</a>/GATE/). Scaffolds within one pseudomolecule were interacted by a stretch of 500 undefined bases (N's). Annotation was carried out following the pipeline of GACP v7.0 (<a href="http://bioinformatics.genomics.org.cn/bio">http://bioinformatics.genomics.org.cn/bio</a>/annotation.html/) that combines the approach of the <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction on the repeat-masked genome, via protein similarity and transcript reconstruction to build optimal gene models using the PASA and EVM pipelines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2561">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2564">60</a></sup>. All genome assembly and annotation data of W14 and KU50 could been found at <a href="http://www.cassava-genome.cn/data.html">http://www.cassava-genome.cn/data.html</a> with visionV1.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">RNA-Seq and annotation</h3><p>The RNA-Seq reads were <i>de novo</i> assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nature Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2587">61</a></sup> and Velvet-Oases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Zerbino, D. R. &amp; Birney, E. Velvet: Algorithms for de novo short read assembly using de Bruijn graphs. Genome Res. 18, 821–829 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6110#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d34285e2591">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Schulz, M. H., Zerbino, D. R., Vingron, M. &amp; Birney, E. Oases: robust de novo RNA-seq assembly across the dynamic range of expression levels.
